  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

</script>

<span id="freeTextContainer2782106322096690896">الطنطورية (نسبة الى قرية الطنطورة الواقعة على الساحل الفلسطيني جنوب حيفا) ، تعرضت هذه القرية عام 1948 لمذبحة على يد العصابات الصهيونية ، تتناول الرواية هذه المذبحة كمنطلق و حدث من الاحداث الرئيسية ، لتتابع حياة عائلة اقتلعت من القرية وحياتها عبر ما يقرب من نصف قرن إلى الآن مرورًا بتجربة اللجوء في لبنان.<br/>بطلة الرواية هي امرأة من القرية يتابع القارئ حياتها منذ الصبا الى الشيخ</span>
  <span id="freeText2782106322096690896" style="display:none">الطنطورية (نسبة الى قرية الطنطورة الواقعة على الساحل الفلسطيني جنوب حيفا) ، تعرضت هذه القرية عام 1948 لمذبحة على يد العصابات الصهيونية ، تتناول الرواية هذه المذبحة كمنطلق و حدث من الاحداث الرئيسية ، لتتابع حياة عائلة اقتلعت من القرية وحياتها عبر ما يقرب من نصف قرن إلى الآن مرورًا بتجربة اللجوء في لبنان.<br />بطلة الرواية هي امرأة من القرية يتابع القارئ حياتها منذ الصبا الى الشيخوخة.<br />الرواية تمزج في سطورها بين الوقائع التاريخية من ناحية و الإبداع الأدبي من ناحية أخرى.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/634178023" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/634178023?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/24366745-the-woman-from-tantoura">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=novels">novels</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer634178023" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16010326665929570942"><b>الطنطورية = The Woman from Tantoura, Radwa Ashour</b><br/><br/>The Woman from Tantoura: A Novel of Palestine. Palestine for most of us, the word brings to mind a series of confused images and disjointed associations-massacres, refugee camps, UN resolutions, settlements, terrorist attacks, war, occupation, checkered kouffiyehs and suicide bombers, a seemingly endless cycle of death and destruction. <br/><br/>This novel does not shy away from such painful images, but it is first and foremost a powerful human story, follo</span>
  <span id="freeText16010326665929570942" style="display:none"><b>الطنطورية = The Woman from Tantoura, Radwa Ashour</b><br /><br />The Woman from Tantoura: A Novel of Palestine. Palestine for most of us, the word brings to mind a series of confused images and disjointed associations-massacres, refugee camps, UN resolutions, settlements, terrorist attacks, war, occupation, checkered kouffiyehs and suicide bombers, a seemingly endless cycle of death and destruction. <br /><br />This novel does not shy away from such painful images, but it is first and foremost a powerful human story, following the life of a young girl from her days in the village of al-Tantoura in Palestine up to the dawn of the new century. <br /><br />We participate in events as they unfold, seeing them through the uneducated but sharply intelligent mind of Ruqayya, as she tries to make sense of all that has happened to her and her family. With her, we live her love of her land and of her people; we feel the repeated pain of loss, of diaspora and of cross-generational misunderstanding; and above all, we come to know her indomitable human spirit. <br /><br />As we read we discover that we have become part of Ruqayya's family, and her voice will remain with us long after we have closed the book.<br /><br />The Woman from Tantoura: A Palestinian Novel, by Radwa Ashour, Translated by: Kay Heikkinen, Publisher: American University in Cairo Press, 2014, ISBN: 1617975710, 9781617975714, Length: 272 pages, Subjects: Fiction, Historical, General<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز بیست و چهارم ماه ژوئن سال 2016میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: الطنطورية (زنی از طنتورا)؛ نویسنده: رضوى عاشور؛<br /><br />با خوانش واژه ی «فلسطین»، برای بسیاری، واژه هایی همچون «کشتار دسته جمعی، اردوگاههای پناهندگان، قطعنامه های سازمان ملل متحد، شهرک ها، حملات تروریستی، جنگ، اشغال، بمب گذاران انتحاری»، یک چرخه ی بی پایان، از مرگ، و نابودی، به دیده مینشیند؛ این رمان از چنین تصاویر دردناکی شرمنده نیست، داستان انسانی ست، زندگی یک دختر جوان است، از روزهای بگذشته بر او، در روستای «الطنتورا» در «فلسطین»، تا سپیده دم سده ی تازه ی میلادی<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 26/07/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="16010326665929570942" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1160668061" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1160668061" />
    <a title="Zanna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna"><img alt="Zanna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512813535p2/22591633.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1160668061?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zanna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zanna" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna">Zanna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789774166150" href="/book/show/17678418-the-woman-from-tantoura">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=bechdel-pass">bechdel-pass</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=2015year-reading-women">2015year-reading-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=500mab">500mab</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=translated">translated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1160668061" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5964951440851049986">This narrative opens with a gloriously ambiguous, searingly romantic image that heralds a lyrical portrait of life in the narrator's idyllic home town on the Palestinian coast. The seeds of a story are sown and I joyfully anticipate a woman-centred tale of love, tradition and modernity set in this paradise and told in the voice of a poet. By whetting our appetite for this tale, by showing that it would be worth the telling as well as worth the living, Ashour imparts bitter anguish and loss when </span>
  <span id="freeText5964951440851049986" style="display:none">This narrative opens with a gloriously ambiguous, searingly romantic image that heralds a lyrical portrait of life in the narrator's idyllic home town on the Palestinian coast. The seeds of a story are sown and I joyfully anticipate a woman-centred tale of love, tradition and modernity set in this paradise and told in the voice of a poet. By whetting our appetite for this tale, by showing that it would be worth the telling as well as worth the living, Ashour imparts bitter anguish and loss when that future is torn away by the <i>Nakba</i>. That the witness and victim whose eyes we peer through on the Catastrophe is a fourteen year old girl allows the full emotional impact of destroyed hopes to crash into my heart, while the more gruesome facts, the historical facts, that the people of the town were turned out of their houses by soldiers who stole valuables, lined men up and slaughtered them, are numbly reported, too impossibly horrific to be conveyed by adjectives.<br /><br />I found the realism of Ruqayya's narrative breathtaking, and caught myself constantly forgetting that it is fiction. Part of what gives it the fire of truth is the reality of the events that shape it, never as a backdrop, but as hubs and spokes of relationships and hooks and poles of the warp and weft of daily life where Ashour maintains her foci. Realism is also created through Ruqqaya's patchy, selective recall, which so exactly resembles geniune memoir. However, this apparently simple, though brilliantly deployed and effective device, is counterpointed and complicated by stylistic and structural complexity.<br /><br />For example, without warning, only a few pages into the novel, we are with Ruqayya as a grandmother, telling stories and describing an annual gathering, in a lucid, conversational style distinct from the sonorous, charged sections of recall. In this scene, she also abruptly answers several of the broad questions about her own future that the initial scenes, particularly the funny and touching recollection of her mother's fears, had set me wondering about. Narrative convention led me to expect a measured, ponderous unfolding of these plot points; but this is not allowed to happen, the plot is severed by the occupation like lives and limbs. <br /><br />In my opinion, the power of this work is that Ashour refuses the occupation - she insistently draws us (not uncritically) back to life, to the gendered spaces of domesticity, to an inner world of contemplation. There is a passage of description of the sun setting over the sea in Tantoura at the start of the novel that is extraordinarily gorgeous and original, where Ruqayya reads and paints the landscape full of ambivalence, creating a shimmering, fragmentary surface that reflects the agitation of the novel's timeline and subject, but also itself forms a break in the flow of the plot as dictated by the violent agenda of an occupying force. This fiercely beautiful interlude underlines the endurance of nature and Palestinians' relationships with the land. The text itself, like the reiteration of memory, is an act of resistance. Indeed, telling the 'female' perspective is resistance, since the social domain embodies and reproduces that which is worth fighting for in the right of return. I was reminded of this <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aKucPh9xHtM" rel="nofollow noopener">video</a> of Rafeef Ziadeh speaking her poem 'we teach life, sir'<br /><br />Presenting her novel as a memoir unwillingly written by an intelligent but not highly educated woman whose violently exiled life contains, alongside unspeakable suffering and grief, many joys and countless acts of love, care, friendship, generosity, Ashour blazingly illuminates the will to endure and live above and beyond the misery of waiting, above and beyond survival. She questions the emotional truth of Homer's account of Penelope waiting for Odysseus - nobody, she declares, would undo the work of social reproduction painstakingly woven. The fruits of her own labours are dazzling; she somehow raises three brilliant, idiosyncratic sons and then a daughter who startles her constantly with her intelligence and maturity. I could not hold back my tears at the close of this vivid portrait of a life that cannot be occupied by hatred and death, that is outlined in love and defiant hope like a glittering constellation, lighting the way home.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5964951440851049986" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hagar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hagar" href="/user/show/1813700-hagar">Hagar</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer11298723863613233924">finished it at 6 am!<br/>Finished reading it in three days!<br/>It's a book you cannot feel but missing it when you turn the last page!<br/><br/>"El Tantoria" is a memory between your hands, a full history, abundance of names and incidents that carve their ways into your memory as well, and you cannot feel but you're part of this collective memory! Rdwa Ashour has narrated, and gave your the burden - the happy burden - the responsibility of carrying these stories - and thus this history and memory- along in your li</span>
  <span id="freeText11298723863613233924" style="display:none">finished it at 6 am!<br />Finished reading it in three days!<br />It's a book you cannot feel but missing it when you turn the last page!<br /><br />"El Tantoria" is a memory between your hands, a full history, abundance of names and incidents that carve their ways into your memory as well, and you cannot feel but you're part of this collective memory! Rdwa Ashour has narrated, and gave your the burden - the happy burden - the responsibility of carrying these stories - and thus this history and memory- along in your life. You cannot feel but it's part of your identity..and your own history... and that you too belong to a place called "Al Tantoria" in Palestine.<br /><br />Radwa Ashour again masters her narrative techniques..and takes you along the seaside..to smell the breeze in Tantoria, Beirut, Seida, Alexandria, Greece, and even in Abu Dhabi. Her narration flows smoothly from the conscious to the unconscious, from past to present, and jumps to the future in one page..and you cannot feel being disrupted...you just go with the flow of her words, short sentences, and subtle words.<br /><br />Moreover, you'll feel your heart aches,and tears seeing the martyrs, hearing the missiles, and destruction, and you'll jump with joy -like real - joy when the family hears good news..as if you're one of this family...and definitely will laugh along with them...when a joke or a reminescence of a joyful family incident is narrated.<br /><br />You cannot feel but anything but part of this family...the microcosm - or may be the everything as well- of Palestine. You cannot finish that book but feeling you're Palestinian as well (aren't we all in a way or another??)..and you too carry the key to your home "el darr" in Palestine... upon your chest- and shoulders.<br /><br />Though again it's the idea of narration of history through a family - as she did in Trilogy of Ghernata- however, you can feel the difference. And you'll raise your hats off to a new masterpiece by Radwa Ashour.<br /><br />A definite book to read, to enjoy, and to inspire and bedazzle you!<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_1142267764" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1142267764" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1142267764?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789774166150" href="/book/show/17678418-the-woman-from-tantoura">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-everything">person-of-everything</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-translated">reality-translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-all">antidote-think-twice-all</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-read">antidote-think-twice-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-translated">person-of-translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-check">reality-check</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-reality-translated">person-of-reality-translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-translated">antidote-translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-reality">person-of-reality</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1142267764" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15042946602000006612"><blockquote>
  <i>No one undoes their weaving even if it looks that way. No one is frozen in the act of waiting.</i>
</blockquote>Once upon a time, I called the vein of literature I am most likely going to be found trawling in escapist. It's taken me many a book and many a bookish aftermath to realize that reading which entrenches one even further into the slaughtering mandated and maintained by a hegemonic reality is not an escape. A more accurate term would be recentering literature, two pages in landing you in a place whose</span>
  <span id="freeText15042946602000006612" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>No one undoes their weaving even if it looks that way. No one is frozen in the act of waiting.</i>
</blockquote>Once upon a time, I called the vein of literature I am most likely going to be found trawling in escapist. It's taken me many a book and many a bookish aftermath to realize that reading which entrenches one even further into the slaughtering mandated and maintained by a hegemonic reality is not an escape. A more accurate term would be recentering literature, two pages in landing you in a place whose nearly ubiquitous locale is states of objecthood and abject. I can't tell you how many thousands of miles and travel are between me and Tantoura, or Tantura, or Palestine, leastwise not under the power of a Google search modification that repeatedly tells and warns and demands that these places do not exist. There are always ways around this, but inaccessibility murders like nothing else. I am sure there are myriad voices such as Ashour's in languages I do not understand, but when the ones I do call in majority for wholesale annihilation and are favored enough by the gods to see it enacted to varying extents, guess at what rate the voices I don't but absolutely need to understand go silent in all languages and from all points of view.<blockquote>
  <i>Who told him to oppress people and serve in an army of occupation?</i>
</blockquote>Of all the abstract conceptualizations, this side and vision and that side of space, I've formed over many a year for my academia and pop culture disposal, the one for the twentieth century is both the easiest and the hardest to work with, not least for my having existed for less than a tenth of it. WWI goes here, Vietnam War goes there, tech boom over this way, flappers over yonder, Harlem Renaissance and Wounded Knee Incident and Iran-Contra affair as the result of determined expansion. It's not hard to flip all that over by going on the other side of the globe and only paying attention to Europe when it's the origin of the sanctioned murderer next door. What's hard is to grapple with a post-WWII decision that's been a game of now-you-see-the-war-crimes-now-you-don't of such historical/cultural/international scale that the time between when I first heard the words Palestine and Israel and the time I discovered what they meant was twenty years, if not more.<blockquote>
  <i>She thinks that they threw them there so it would not be said that they killed children in the hospital.</i>
</blockquote>In terms of "sides", my stance is rather simple. You think genocide is quite alright under x number of circumstances, or you don't. You're alright with your opinions having body counts, or you're not. The first person POV of a Palestinian woman who lived through the first origin of Nakba and on doesn't suddenly make me Palestinian, or Arab, or Jewish. What I, a white Euro raised Catholic actually atheist through and through, need to remember is that there are Palestinian Jewish people, and that my witnessing of the trend of massacres this work moves through does not preclude my witnessing of antisemitism and anti-Arab racism and all this duo's incest in my own country. If there are two antagonists in a long and bloody issue, I'd imagine refraining from targeting both as only a white supremacist military industrial complex can do would do some good for their dialectic.<blockquote>
  <i>What logic is there in my running after the memory that has escaped, trying to flee from itself? Do I want to kill it so that I can live, or am I trying to revive it even if I die because...because why? I suddenly scream: Damn memory, damn its mother and father, damn the sky over it and the day it was and the day it will be. Damn the flies!</i>
</blockquote>Now. The writing. I usually pass over this because the politics I insist on getting first through exhaust my usual tanks of compositional energy, but here I have to make the effort for whatever reason. Being a writer myself, I recognize my capitalistically beneficial bias of liking writing similar to my own, current or potential or somewhere in between. It may be the I, I, I doing it, but that passage above is as different from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10210.Jane_Eyre" title="Jane Eyre by Charlotte Brontë" rel="nofollow noopener">Jane Eyre</a> in ways that cannot be subsumed by, oh. They were women of their times. If you read this book, you'll no longer recognize your time, because you'll be too lost in the repetition of generations in name and martyrdom and the healing of a wound that is continually fresh, continually soldered, continually wrenched and stitched and gutted and tourniqueted and amputated, but that last will never be for good so long as works like these are written.<blockquote>
  <i>Mama, sometimes we keep things without being able to sum up their value in one meaning.</i>
</blockquote>If anyone gives me shit about my social justice, I'll simply respond that I am a human being of my times.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15042946602000006612" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1142115941?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ReemK10 (Paper Pills)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ReemK10 (Paper Pills)" href="/user/show/15726428-reemk10-paper-pills">ReemK10 (Paper Pills)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789774166150" href="/book/show/17678418-the-woman-from-tantoura">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1142115941" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18261574776997427027">As a review, I offer this thread from The Year of Reading Women -2015<br/><br/><br/>Click here: 2015: The Year of Reading Women - A's: Radwa Ashour <br/><br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/2135368-radwa-ashour?order=a&page=1'>https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/...</a><br/> <br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/2135368-radwa-ashour?order=a&page=2'>http://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/2...</a><br/> <br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/2135368-radwa-ashour?order=a&page=3'>http://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/2...</a><br/><br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/2135368-radwa-ashour?order=a&page=4'>http://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/2...</a><br/><br/>My deepest thanks and gratitude to Zanna, Ce Ce, Kalliope, Laima, Marcelita and Sue for being a part of this read!! <br/><br/><br/>For the links that did not go through because of connection problems:<br/><br/>That is a really goo</span>
  <span id="freeText18261574776997427027" style="display:none">As a review, I offer this thread from The Year of Reading Women -2015<br /><br /><br />Click here: 2015: The Year of Reading Women - A's: Radwa Ashour <br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/2135368-radwa-ashour?order=a&amp;page=1">https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/...</a><br /> <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/2135368-radwa-ashour?order=a&amp;page=2">http://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/2...</a><br /> <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/2135368-radwa-ashour?order=a&amp;page=3">http://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/2...</a><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/2135368-radwa-ashour?order=a&amp;page=4">http://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/2...</a><br /><br />My deepest thanks and gratitude to Zanna, Ce Ce, Kalliope, Laima, Marcelita and Sue for being a part of this read!! <br /><br /><br />For the links that did not go through because of connection problems:<br /><br />That is a really good question Zanna! Isn't it wonderful that Radwa Ashour mentions the gypsies in her novel? I asked my parents and they do remember the presence of gypsies. They remember them as being very colorful, wore lots of make up as well as a lot of jewelry, especially known for hoop earrings. They were always on the move, and there weren't very many of them. My mother remembers them as showing up around the Eid celebrations begging for money. My father remembers buying metal trinkets from them. Parents would warn their children to avoid them as there was the fear that they would kidnap children.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.flamencobeirut.com/album/preview_gal/15-Gypsies_Palestine.jpg">http://www.flamencobeirut.com/album/p...</a><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://rlv.zcache.com/gypsy_women_in_palestine_1893_poster-rcc57d4d62c5e4f1dafa9e183918e3d6c_aisam_8byvr_324.jpg">http://rlv.zcache.com/gypsy_women_in_...</a><br /><br />The English term "Gypsy" is derived from the word "Egypt," based on a misassumption that the Gypsies' origin is found in this corner of North Africa. There are Gypsies in Egypt, but they migrated from Palestine, and originally came from India.<br /><br />The Gypsies of Palestine call themselves Dom, which means "man" in their native Domari language. In Arabic, also a native language for this community, they are called Nawar, which derives from the word for fire. They may have been called this because many of them worked as blacksmiths in the past.<br /><br />Today, Dom Gypsy communities live in many of the towns, villages and refugee camps of the West Bank and Gaza Strip. Although the estimates vary depending on who is doing the guesswork, there may be roughly 1,000 Gypsies living throughout the West Bank (not including Jerusalem) and as many as three times that number in the Gaza Strip.<br /><br />There are also an estimated 1,000 Gypsies living in Jerusalem's Old City, Ras al-Amud, Silwan and other neighbourhoods. Suffering the fate of other Palestinians, many more live as refugees in neighbouring countries, particularly Jordan. A people within a people, these Gypsy communities share a unique heritage that connects them to a global Gypsy Diaspora, even as they identify with the more contemporary history and culture of being Palestinian.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.domresearchcenter.com/news/israel/isra7.html">http://www.domresearchcenter.com/news...</a><br /><br />Click here: Roma in Israel <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.domresearchcenter.com/news/israel/isra7.html">http://www.domresearchcenter.com/news...</a><br /> <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://archive.thisweekinpalestine.com/details.php?id=2209&amp;ed=144&amp;edid=144">http://archive.thisweekinpalestine.co...</a><br />-------------------------------------------------------------------------Ashour writes, " The massacres depicted in the novel are documented events: the massacres of Tantoura, of Sabra and Shatila, of the shelter in the children's school in Sidon, of the Jad building and others."<br /><br />Part 3: Professor Ilan Pappe reveals the truth about the 1948 Arab-Israeli War and the ethnic cleansing of Palestine<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=T9sHZjYtFig&amp;feature=related">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=T9sHZ...</a><br />-----------------------------------------------------------------<br /><br />"We will not permit the Palestinians to live on Lebanese land." P. 206<br /><br /> Let Beirut be what it wants to be: <br /> This, our blood raised high for her, <br /> Is an unbending tree. I now wish, <br /> I wish I knew where the heart will fly, <br /> That I may release for her the bird of my heart <br /> And from my body let him set me free. <br /> <br /> <br /><br /> Here, within her, I live, <br /> A banner from my own shroud. <br /> Here, I leave behind what’s not mine. <br /> And here, I dive into my own soul, <br /> That my time may start with me. <br /> Let Beirut be what it wants to be. <br /> She will forget me, <br /> That I may forget her. <br /> <br /><br /> Will I forget? Oh, would, oh, would I could <br /> This moment bring back my homeland <br /> Out of myself! I wish I knew what I desire <br /> I wish I knew! <br /> I wish I knew! <br /><br /> <br />Mahmoud Darwish Memory for Forgetfulness <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://publishing.cdlib.org/ucpressebooks/view?docId=ft1z09n7g7;brand=ucpress">http://publishing.cdlib.org/ucpresseb...</a><br />--------------------------------------------------------------------------<br /><br />"When the Israelis entered Beirut nine months earlier they stormed into the Center and stole half of its library and archives." P.209<br />This reminded me of The Great Book Robbery 70,000 Palestinian books were systematically "collected" by the newly created State of Israel during the 1948 war. The story of the "collected" books is at the heart of our film. <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://thegreatbookrobbery.org/">http://thegreatbookrobbery.org/</a><br />--------------------------------------------------------------------------<br /><br />"Wisal laughed and spread her fingers in his face. "Five and five again! I'll put a spell on her to protect her from your jealous eyes." p. 248<br /> <br />Five and five is khamsa we khmaisa <br /> <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://i.ebayimg.com/00/s/OTk3WDE1MDA=/z/W8cAAMXQAx9RXWFq/$T2eC16NHJHIE9nyseG53BR(WFp5gZQ~~60_35.JPG">http://i.ebayimg.com/00/s/OTk3WDE1MDA...</a><br /> <br />A blue or green eye can also be found on some forms of the hamsa hand, an apotropaic hand-shaped talisman against the evil eye found in West Asia. The word hamsa, also spelled khamsa and hamesh, means "five" referring to the fingers of the hand.<br />--------------------------------------------------------------------------<br /><br />"Not a day goes by but that I remember a proverb she quoted or a scene she was part of." P 304<br />This is so true of the Arabic language. Proverbial language is very typical of Arabic. <br />A Collection of Palestinian Proverbs: "Elli beitoh min qzaaz ma birmi enaas be7jaar" (He who has a house of glass throws no people with stones.")<br /> <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://abualkam1982.ahlamontada.com/t55-a-collection-of-palestinian-proverbs">http://abualkam1982.ahlamontada.com/t...</a><br />--------------------------------------------------------------------------<br /><br />"As we approached we were greeted by the aroma of the oven-baked bread and the mixture of onions, sumac and olive oil, and suddenly I said, "I'm hungry!" p. 304<br /> <br />Musakhan (Arabic: مسخّن‎) is a Palestinian dish composed of roasted chicken baked with onions,sumac, allspice, saffron, and fried pine nuts served over taboon bread. It is also known as Muhammar (Arabic: محمر‎).<br /><br />The dish is simple to make and the ingredients needed are easily obtainable, which may account for the dish's popularity. Many of the ingredients used: olive oil, sumac and pine nuts are frequently found in Palestinian cuisine.<br /><br />Musakhan is a dish that one typically eats with one's hands. It is usually presented with the chicken on top of the bread, and could be served with soup. The term 'musakhan' literally means "something that is heated."[1]<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Musakhan">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Musakhan</a><br /><br />--------------------------------------------------------------------------<br /><br />Another very typical Palestinian dish is the maqlouba<br /><br />"There was no better musakhan than Umm Jaamil's , nor any better maqlouba than hers.: p, 304<br /><br />Maqluba (Arabic: مقلوبة‎; Turkish: maklube;) is a traditional dish of the Arab Levant,.[1] The dish includes meat, rice, and fried vegetables placed in a pot, which is then flipped upside down when served, hence the name maqluba, which translates literally as "upside-down".<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maqluba">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maqluba</a><br /><br />--------------------------------------------------------------------------<br /><br />Asmahan P. 311<br /><br />Amal al-Atrash (Arabic: آمال الأطرش‎ Āmāl al-Aṭrash; 25 November 1912 – 14 July 1944),[1] better known by her stage name Asmahan (أسمهان Asmahān), was an Arab Druze singer and actress of Syrian origins who lived in Egypt. Having immigrated to Egypt in childhood, her family knew the composerDawood Hosni, and she sang the compositions of Mohamed El Qasabgi and Zakariyya Ahmad.[2][3] She also sang the compositions of Mohammed Abdel Wahab and her brother Farid al-Atrash, a then rising star musician in his own right. Hers was the only female voice in Arab music to pose serious competition to that of Umm Kulthum,[4] who is considered to be one of the Arab world's most distinguished singers of the 20th century.[5] Her mysterious death in an automobile accident shocked the public. Journalists spread gossip about her turbulent personal life and an alleged espionage role in World War II.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asmahan">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asmahan</a><br /><br />--------------------------------------------------------------------------<br /><br />"The longer story was about three Palestinians who wanted to be smuggled into Kuwait, so they hid in the empty tank of a water truck. The border employees delayed the driver so the three died of suffocation, without daring to knock on the sides of the tank." P. 320<br /><br /> <br />Men in the Sun and Other Palestinian Stories<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.amazon.com/Men-Sun-Other-Palestinian-Stories/dp/0894108573">http://www.amazon.com/Men-Sun-Other-P...</a><br /><br />--------------------------------------------------------------------------<br /><br />"I'll go back, like him. Not on the back of a donkey but by the logic of birds." p.330 " Do the pictures nourish the logic of bhe birds?" p.332<br /><br />The logic of birds or Manteq al Tayr. <br /><br />This illustrated manuscript of Farid al-Din cAttar's mystical poem Mantiq al-Tayr (Language of the Birds) is one of the most important illustrated manuscripts from Timurid Persia (1370–1507) and a highlight of the Islamic collection at the Metropolitan Museum of Art.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/hd/mant/hd_mant.htm">http://www.metmuseum.org/toah/hd/mant...</a><br /><br />--------------------------------------------------------------------------<br /><br />I thank you all for reading The Woman of Tantoura. I dedicate this to the late Radwa Ashour. Yours, Reem<br /><br />In your absence<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=pJhPZ-dAYQk">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=pJhPZ...</a><br /><br /><br /></span>

    <a title="Claire" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2528841-claire"><img alt="Claire" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1336501182p2/2528841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3466006919?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Claire" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Claire" href="/user/show/2528841-claire">Claire</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789774166150" href="/book/show/17678418-the-woman-from-tantoura">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=around-the-world">around-the-world</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=translated">translated</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3466006919" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2219657875681483239">The more I read, the more this novel got its hooks into me. Because of the initial setting, a village in Palestine, Tantoura in 1948, we know that it's going to be traumatic, it's the part we don't wish to linger in, to be witness to the horrific expulsion of families from their homes, of the disappearance and massacre of the sons and fathers. <br/><br/>And even if we know, sometimes, we need to read through, to follow the lives of a thirteen year old girl, to see her find some solace, to be understanding</span>
  <span id="freeText2219657875681483239" style="display:none">The more I read, the more this novel got its hooks into me. Because of the initial setting, a village in Palestine, Tantoura in 1948, we know that it's going to be traumatic, it's the part we don't wish to linger in, to be witness to the horrific expulsion of families from their homes, of the disappearance and massacre of the sons and fathers. <br /><br />And even if we know, sometimes, we need to read through, to follow the lives of a thirteen year old girl, to see her find some solace, to be understanding of her worrying and anxiety for her own children and husband. To see them seek refuge in another country, where a similar tragedy can happen, one understands what it must feel like to think that no place is safe, and then when she finds the 'safe place' with her son, the culture shock is too much. It brings to mind the title of the memoir of the Palestinian intellectual Edward Said's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22136.Out_of_Place" title="Out of Place by Edward W. Said" rel="nofollow noopener">Out of Place</a>.<br /><br />I found similarities to my recent reading of Nella Larsen's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/78257.Quicksand" title="Quicksand by Nella Larsen" rel="nofollow noopener">Quicksand</a>, in her constant search too, for the place where one can be oneself, though hers perhaps all the more elusive, because it is not so much a place or a people, she represents in her mixed blood, something that the clannishness of many 'peoples' rejects. <br /><br />Much more to write on this, allowing the thoughts to percolate and the many, many passages highlighted to reread.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2219657875681483239" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1736736588" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9833424844842282589">This book has taken me aback and its story has stuck so deep in my mind, that led me to an obsessive search for people, and places and dates. I ended up finding an interesting (and infuriating) story about an academic whose masters thesis was rejected because he dared to tell the Tantoura's massacre story, Teddy Katz (more about it <a href='https://mondoweiss.net/2016/03/the-tantura-massacre-of-1948-and-the-academic-character-assassination-of-teddy-katz/?fbclid=IwAR2pQmtNxG1NBWGObLa0sV1Ywshkh73hWmpH-138E9QqJ0tZ1a_RebDF_dQ' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a>). <br/>I've been looking for remains of Tantoura on Google Maps, and the little I've found makes me think how successful Israel has been on covering it's army's war c</span>
  <span id="freeText9833424844842282589" style="display:none">This book has taken me aback and its story has stuck so deep in my mind, that led me to an obsessive search for people, and places and dates. I ended up finding an interesting (and infuriating) story about an academic whose masters thesis was rejected because he dared to tell the Tantoura's massacre story, Teddy Katz (more about it <a href="https://mondoweiss.net/2016/03/the-tantura-massacre-of-1948-and-the-academic-character-assassination-of-teddy-katz/?fbclid=IwAR2pQmtNxG1NBWGObLa0sV1Ywshkh73hWmpH-138E9QqJ0tZ1a_RebDF_dQ" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>). <br />I've been looking for remains of Tantoura on Google Maps, and the little I've found makes me think how successful Israel has been on covering it's army's war crimes (some historians, like Ilan Pappé, claims that it was part of an ethnical cleansing plan).<br />I have nothing to do with Israel versus Palestine issues, and everything I knew about it was the few news spread by Brazilian TV, mostly based on US news, so I just suspected that I got very biased information. I just had no idea. This is what this book has done for me, it opened my eyes and gave me a new perspective.<br />Besides the rich and informative plunge in history, it is a beautiful story of a woman, her memories, her struggles and her beautiful life, a story of someone who finds the strength to carry on despite the stain in her soul left by the massacre of her people and the denying of her people's story. Ruqayya is a wonderful character, her memories show not only the trauma but also the flowers that still can grow on the ravaged ground.<br /><br />#21 #Palestine #traveling-the-world-through-books</span>

<div id="review_2535096452" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2535096452" />
    <a title="Miriam Cihodariu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51872363-miriam-cihodariu"><img alt="Miriam Cihodariu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542966196p2/51872363.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2535096452?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Miriam Cihodariu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Miriam Cihodariu" href="/user/show/51872363-miriam-cihodariu">Miriam Cihodariu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789774166150" href="/book/show/17678418-the-woman-from-tantoura">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51872363-miriam-cihodariu?shelf=palestina">palestina</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2535096452" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6447886388397057444">This reads gently, tenderly, and is written unhurriedly, just like a true memoir of an old lady fondly remembering her family and all the little facts her memory jumps to and divagates to in the midst of another story. It's one of the reasons for which the story feels incredibly authentic, so real that you can hardly believe the main characters are actually fictional. Such writing skill deserves nothing short of 5 stars. <br/><br/>The relaxed pace and the regard which envelopes all things with a gentle am</span>
  <span id="freeText6447886388397057444" style="display:none">This reads gently, tenderly, and is written unhurriedly, just like a true memoir of an old lady fondly remembering her family and all the little facts her memory jumps to and divagates to in the midst of another story. It's one of the reasons for which the story feels incredibly authentic, so real that you can hardly believe the main characters are actually fictional. Such writing skill deserves nothing short of 5 stars. <br /><br />The relaxed pace and the regard which envelopes all things with a gentle amount of sunny attention before it drifts off to the next tiny thing to be observed, like in a reverie - this style reminds me greatly of Marilynne Robinson's Gilead trilogy, even if the topics are not very similar.<br /><br />At the same time, we are constantly reminded that this is a story which goes beyond the family saga of the main characters. It's the story of generalized PTSD and of constant exile, of people moving from place to place and holding on to their original roots with a soul-shattering fervor. One of the images which struck me the most was that of entire generations of women who discover a common secret among themselves, regardless of whether they live in camps or are scattered throughout the world - they are all wearing the key to their house (from which they were evacuated) on a string around their necks. Even if decades passed since they were driven away from their homes. This stubborn hope is beautiful and tragic. <br /><br />In many moments when people react awkwardly to one another, you sometimes can't tell if it's the usual way in which family ties get complicated and multi-layered, or if it's the PTSD manifesting itself. It's sad regardless of the explanation, and it's probably meant to be something in between.<br /><br />I like that the story doesn't even attempt to be objective or balanced when describing the evils of the Jewish-Palestinian conflict. I am trying to see past sides in it, so I tend to believe that the truth is again, in between, but a book such as this one is infinitely valuable precisely because it presents facts so utterly through a one-sided prism. <br /><br />We have so few voices coming in from that side of the conflict that a subjective account is precious like a jewel. I, for one, welcomed and enjoyed it - mostly for the family relationships and the world described, but for the historic and social value as well. <br /><br />---------------------------------------------------<br />“Don’t run away from the promise.”<br />“I’ll try, Hasan. But what if I die? The writing will kill me.”<br />“It won’t kill you, you’re stronger than you think. Memory does not kill. It inflicts unbearable pain, perhaps; but we bear it, and memory changes from a whirlpool that pulls us to the bottom, to a sea we can swim in. We cover distances, we control it, and we dictate to it.”<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6447886388397057444" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1593935799" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1593935799" />
    <a title="Ayala Levinger" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/34793354-ayala-levinger"><img alt="Ayala Levinger" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1410252915p2/34793354.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1593935799?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ayala Levinger" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ayala Levinger" href="/user/show/34793354-ayala-levinger">Ayala Levinger</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789774166150" href="/book/show/17678418-the-woman-from-tantoura">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1593935799" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11173524707718923234">Ruqayya was 14 years old when she and her family were forced to leave Palestine on the night of the massacre of Tantoura, a village on the Paelstinian coast with its natural wave breaking islands. <br/>This same beach was a favorite vacation resort for my family when I was groing up and and none of us bothered to learn the history of the village that was destoyed by zionist forces 27,5 years before I was born, when nobody of my family was even considering immigrating to Palestine. <br/><br/>Told in the first p</span>
  <span id="freeText11173524707718923234" style="display:none">Ruqayya was 14 years old when she and her family were forced to leave Palestine on the night of the massacre of Tantoura, a village on the Paelstinian coast with its natural wave breaking islands. <br />This same beach was a favorite vacation resort for my family when I was groing up and and none of us bothered to learn the history of the village that was destoyed by zionist forces 27,5 years before I was born, when nobody of my family was even considering immigrating to Palestine. <br /><br />Told in the first person point of view, Ruqayya is telling the story of her exile. It is like reading a diary, some every day life matters, some family events and some actual historical events that touched her life. She tries to tell her story chronollogically but sometimes she naturally dwells and then stops and start over. It is charming and reads very natural.<br />What also natural is that not all the Palestinian refugees think and want the same. for example, her older son just wants to make a very good living and not be constantly reminded he is a refugee and who could blame him? the younger son seems dellusioned with the idea of taking the criminals responsible to massacres of Palestinian villages and refugee camps in Leabanon, to court, using holes in the laws in Europian countries (just before ICC existed) and Ruqayya, still all this time and everywhere in her journey carrying a cord with the key to her house in Tantoura but I had the whole time the feeling she knows returning home will not happen in her lifetime. <br /><br />This book is fiction based on accurate real events (like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6692041.Mornings_in_Jenin" title="Mornings in Jenin by Susan Abulhawa" rel="nofollow noopener"> Morning in Jenin</a>, by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5815562.Susan_Abulhawa" title="Susan Abulhawa" rel="nofollow noopener"> Susan Abulhawa</a>) which is a very good way to learn about these events (or trigger your curiousity to learn more) without reading academic non-fiction books. It is a family saga but also a story of a whole nation and you know from the beginning it will not have a happy-ever-after ending. I read it with tears in my throat that occasionaly burst out and the end was reading between the tears but it is not a depressing book. It gives hope too.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11173524707718923234" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3466118688" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3466118688" />
    <a title="Lehar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10307436-lehar"><img alt="Lehar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592980759p2/10307436.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3466118688?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lehar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lehar" href="/user/show/10307436-lehar">Lehar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/24366745-the-woman-from-tantoura">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3466118688" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11917707531156691328">I felt dazed after reading it. Perhaps that’s how Ruqayya felt after losing her sweet home, her brothers and father to unspeakable violence, her mother to senility, her husband to militia massacre, her country to invaders, and her children to newer ideologies and distant countries. <br/><br/>In Tantoura, Ruqayya's mother perceived even short distances to be unassailable, but life took Ruqayya to distant places. As Ruqayya changed cities and countries, she boiled over many pots of coffee reminiscing about </span>
  <span id="freeText11917707531156691328" style="display:none">I felt dazed after reading it. Perhaps that’s how Ruqayya felt after losing her sweet home, her brothers and father to unspeakable violence, her mother to senility, her husband to militia massacre, her country to invaders, and her children to newer ideologies and distant countries. <br /><br />In Tantoura, Ruqayya's mother perceived even short distances to be unassailable, but life took Ruqayya to distant places. As Ruqayya changed cities and countries, she boiled over many pots of coffee reminiscing about the beaches in her village, the life she had there, and all the people she had lost. Her shore was plundered but not her love for it. And the only way to recover the loss was by meeting people who traced their roots to the same seaside location. <br /><br />Sea is a recurring motif in late Radwa Ashour’s story. In Beruit, in Alexandria – sea followed Ruqayya everywhere, like the soul of a lost place. The gradual slipping of sand under her feet, the brushing of waves against her ankles it all made Ruqayya pine for Tantoura. As Palestine was sliding into obscurity, Ruqayya and her refugee companions helped it prevail through tales and traditions. <br /><br />Ruqayya was melancholic but brave. She shouldered her responsibilities heroically even when the weight of displacement and loss pressed hard against her chest. Talking to Wisal made her happy - Wisal, her childhood friend, who had also lost her home to the invading forces. For a short period in her life, Ruqayya found solace in Wisal’s brother Abed’s company. They had spent time together in Tantoura, allowing Ruqayya to relive her happy childhood days with him over coffee and conversations.<br /><br />When Hasan, Ruqayya's son, asked her to write the story of her life, she was initially dismissive of the idea. Then one day, she sat down with pen and paper, and the memories assaulted her. The task proved too distressing. Sometimes she did manage to write. Other times she rambled, unable to articulate the emotions that choked her. <br /><br />People find intimate ways to keep the memory of those they’ve lost alive. Ruqayya named two of her children after her brothers. What better way to honour and remember the departed, and heal a broken heart?<br /><br />Maryam, Ruqayya’s daughter, brought some sunshine to the story. She is Ruqayya's antithesis, looking to the future rather than the past. But she perceives her mother's pain and is always willing to do little things to make her happy. Ruqayya's firstborn is Sadiq -pragmatic, kind and dutiful. Hasan is second. He's polite and a scholar with an encouraging word for others and none to express himself. Abed, the youngest among the boys and older than Maryam, is angry and untamed. He channels his fury by directing it towards revolutionary work. Together, they make a lovely cast of characters.<br /><br />The Woman from Tantoura is a page-turner; a beautiful story on life, love, and loss.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11917707531156691328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2372559214" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2372559214" />
    <a title="Farya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47020604-farya"><img alt="Farya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469797682p2/47020604.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2372559214?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Farya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Farya" href="/user/show/47020604-farya">Farya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789774166150" href="/book/show/17678418-the-woman-from-tantoura">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2372559214" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3524640794235569804">Every aspect of the Palestinian experience is tragic, and that tragedy seeps through the pores of everyday life. Through the story of Ruqqaya and her family, we find that no matter how far up or away one climbs, she becomes inexplicably rooted to the memory of home, belonging, and the dream of return. Memory is has played the most powerful defense against the active and constant attempt to annihilate Palestine. The way memory is explored in this novel is wonderful. <br/>The melancholic idea of how ch</span>
  <span id="freeText3524640794235569804" style="display:none">Every aspect of the Palestinian experience is tragic, and that tragedy seeps through the pores of everyday life. Through the story of Ruqqaya and her family, we find that no matter how far up or away one climbs, she becomes inexplicably rooted to the memory of home, belonging, and the dream of return. Memory is has played the most powerful defense against the active and constant attempt to annihilate Palestine. The way memory is explored in this novel is wonderful. <br />The melancholic idea of how changes and transitions physically manifest themselves in the lives of the characters (Tanoura, Beirut, Alexandria, Abu Dhabi, France and Canada), fracturing and separating them to all parts of the world - each marked by tragedy and constantly reconfiguring the the constancy of homeland into their lives. <br />Walking through a remembered terrain only leaves space for mourning the losses of one's civilization (as in the case of Hasan). Palestinians are confronted and attacked time and again in their determination to have their identities and bodies recognised and respected. <br />The homeland becomes an unavoidable longing - there has to be a resolution, but the world offers nothing close to that. So Palestinians fathom hundreds of possible reconciliations - in the hope that one might catalyse that most hungered for of returns.<br />Inshallah, it will happen.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3524640794235569804" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer18335382242947144279">"An unfulfilled desire has no sanctuary other than remembrance."<br/>In this novel, Radwa Ashour tells us the story of Ruqayya who'd witnessed the horrors of the israeli invasion of the lands of Palestine since her young age.   <span id="freeText18335382242947144279" style="display:none">"An unfulfilled desire has no sanctuary other than remembrance."<br />In this novel, Radwa Ashour tells us the story of Ruqayya who'd witnessed the horrors of the israeli invasion of the lands of Palestine since her young age. Ruqayya was forced to leave her home village on the Palestinian coast with her mother when she was 13 (after the murder of her father and brothers by the Israeli armies) in order to escape the war. However, the war has never stopped chasing her wherever she went; the image of ruined houses, hungry refugees in the Lebanese camps, the corpses of her father and brothers haunted her until her old age. She got married, she had 3 sons, and a daughter later on, she lived in Lebanon, in Abu Dhabi and in Cairo, the chain carrying the key to her house in Tantoura and the memory of her village never left her.<br />Oscillating between the present and the past, the author tells us all this in a simple language but with strong emotions that sometimes make us travel to Palestine to smell of the sea of ​​Tantoura and to Sidon at other times to enjoy the scent of the orange blossoms. The accuracy of the detailed historical events around which revolves the story of Ruqayya make you often forget that it's fiction, rather, it'll make you relate to the characters and reflect on many things (war, family, love, independance...).<br />"...you don't need to tell me that we can't bring Palestine back with words!"<br />So grateful to the special one who gifted me this book.</span>

<div id="review_163809709" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/163809709" />
    <a title="Radwa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3973399-radwa"><img alt="Radwa" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/163809709?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Radwa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Radwa" href="/user/show/3973399-radwa">Radwa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer163809709" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17419186858114329888">How many times have words failed to describe what goes on in our minds? Definitely this is one of them, because the uniqueness of this novel makes reviewing it a delicate task: what to praise and underline and what to leave to the reader to discover? Talk about the story line, the historic and historical figures, events, and details it describes, the seamlessly flowing narration, the depth and insight it delivers, the truths it reveals...etc.<br/><br/>AlTantoureyyah is the story of Palestine over the past</span>
  <span id="freeText17419186858114329888" style="display:none">How many times have words failed to describe what goes on in our minds? Definitely this is one of them, because the uniqueness of this novel makes reviewing it a delicate task: what to praise and underline and what to leave to the reader to discover? Talk about the story line, the historic and historical figures, events, and details it describes, the seamlessly flowing narration, the depth and insight it delivers, the truths it reveals...etc.<br /><br />AlTantoureyyah is the story of Palestine over the past 62 years and more, told through the tale of Ruqayya, a woman from Al-Tantoura (a coastal Palestinian village) who has faced and survived it all: the Tantoura massacre that claimed hundreds of lives; the repetitive displacements and alienation; the refugee camps; the continuous deportations; the raids; the massive losses; the untold atrocities; the intense fear and worry; the agony of memories that linger on; and the endless waiting—for an endeared person, a home, a motherland, usurped rights, a future, a life. Through all this, there is a celebration of the existence, a realization of life, an appreciation of every rising sun. There is also a construction of new families, tied not by blood but by similar experiences and shared hardships.<br /><br />The novel is written with such exactness that it carries the readers away to the heart of the events and creates an affinity between them and the characters. It informs and educates a generation whose knowledge of the Palestinian struggle is vague, not only with the geographical and chronological details, but also with the hidden facts underneath, with what might have not been passed down through the decades. AlTantoureyyah bequeaths the legacy of truth so that we won’t forget; a transformational testimony that safeguards history against distortion.<br /><br />AlTantoureyyah—a must read, because words fall short.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17419186858114329888" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <span id="freeText8170982185001774323" style="display:none">What a book and What a history ?!!!<br />Being homeless is painful .. But being homeless in your home is way too more than just painful .. It has all the meanings of humiliation and injustice. <br /><br />This book took me to a very long period over the history since 1936 till 2000 .. Made me start from the very beginning with the Palestinians' misery of losing their country and living all their lives as fugitives.<br /><br />This book took me to this ugly and human-less massacres in Palestine and Lebanon and how frightful the life could be when you hear nothing all the time except about news of people you know are dying here or there, news about others being kidnapped or arrested and what is more frightful than the news is having no news .. Thinking about your absent father, husband or son and you have no idea where is he, you fears take over you and your imaginations go to your worst assumptions.<br /><br />This book is recommended for whomever wants to read about the history of the Palestine case .. But not in a history book !!</span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1543659360?book_show_action=true">Feb 10, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fatma Salim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fatma Salim" href="/user/show/40922975-fatma-salim">Fatma Salim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1543659360" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18113236259560056755">"Amin went to sleep, but I was between sleep and waking all night long. I felt him leave the bed, so I got up. I looked at my watch and it was near to six. He ate a hurried breakfast and kissed Maryam's forehead and his mother's, as they were both deeply asleep. He said as he was taking leave of me at the door, "I don't think that there will be anymore shelling. I'll be home in the evening."<br/><br/>This book follows the life story of Ruqayya, a woman from the Palestinian village of Tantoura. Tantoura be</span>
  <span id="freeText18113236259560056755" style="display:none">"Amin went to sleep, but I was between sleep and waking all night long. I felt him leave the bed, so I got up. I looked at my watch and it was near to six. He ate a hurried breakfast and kissed Maryam's forehead and his mother's, as they were both deeply asleep. He said as he was taking leave of me at the door, "I don't think that there will be anymore shelling. I'll be home in the evening."<br /><br />This book follows the life story of Ruqayya, a woman from the Palestinian village of Tantoura. Tantoura became famous as the site of a massacre by Israeli forces in 1948.<br />The realism of Ruqayya's narrative is breathtaking, and caught myself constantly forgetting that it is fiction. Part of what gives it the fire of truth is the reality of the events that shape it, never as a backdrop, but as hubs and spokes of relationships and hooks of daily life.<br /><br />A novel of #Palestine... with all its peaks and valleys of human experience. I highly recommend it be in your to-read list.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18113236259560056755" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1130157680" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1130157680" />
    <a title="Kate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/536658-kate"><img alt="Kate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267552274p2/536658.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1130157680?book_show_action=true">Dec 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kate" href="/user/show/536658-kate">Kate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789774166150" href="/book/show/17678418-the-woman-from-tantoura">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1130157680" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer845549621855398005">This is a deeply personal historical novel. Though written by the Egyptian author, Radwa Ashour, it takes place in Palestine, starting in 1948 and ending in the 21st century. The main character, a woman from Tantoura, tells the story of her life, of the forced leaving of her family from her village during the Nakba, and the subsequent six homes she lives in in Palestine, Lebanon, UAE, Egypt, finally, in the last chapter returning to Palestine. Because it is written in the first person, and descr</span>
  <span id="freeText845549621855398005" style="display:none">This is a deeply personal historical novel. Though written by the Egyptian author, Radwa Ashour, it takes place in Palestine, starting in 1948 and ending in the 21st century. The main character, a woman from Tantoura, tells the story of her life, of the forced leaving of her family from her village during the Nakba, and the subsequent six homes she lives in in Palestine, Lebanon, UAE, Egypt, finally, in the last chapter returning to Palestine. Because it is written in the first person, and describes what she experienced and her relationship with children, other family members and friends, the story has the feeling of nonfiction, a woman telling a real story of what happened to individuals and families from Palestine, their battles, politics, survival strategies, and dreams of return. It is powerful and intimate (yes, I cried during parts of it). </span>
  <a data-text-id="845549621855398005" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8504587201706416543">It feels like Rouqaya took me with her to all the places she traveled to! However, I would love to hear the opinion of an actual Palestinian who lived through all of the events I read about in الطنطورية. </span>
